**CLARA:**
Okay Mark ‚Äî now that we have the full job offer + non-compete in hand, I can give you a clear, honest read on what‚Äôs in play and what leverage you do (and don‚Äôt) have.

---

### ‚öñÔ∏è TL;DR:
You signed an **at-will employment** agreement with a **Kentucky-based LLC** that includes a **non-compete**, **non-solicit**, and **non-disclosure**, enforceable under **Kentucky law**. You don‚Äôt get PTO protection under Maryland law anymore because SES is based in Kentucky, and that‚Äôs your legal employer.

You **can** file unemployment in either Maryland or Kentucky ‚Äî but Kentucky will likely be the determining state since your employer is KY-based. If you want to **make it painful** for them (in legal cost and time), yes ‚Äî you could raise flags in **both states** and let the inter-agency mess drag them through it. But there are smarter ways to play this depending on your goals.

---

## üß® Worst Case Scenario (for Them):
Let‚Äôs say you want to ‚Äúburn the bridge‚Äù and cause pain.

They could face:
- **Dual-state claims** (Maryland and Kentucky), triggering time-wasting investigations and document submissions to both labor departments.
- **Potential penalty for misclassification** (if you worked largely in MD but were treated as KY).
- **HR scrambling**: They‚Äôd have to defend their termination decision, validate compliance with both MD and KY wage laws, and possibly explain any withheld commissions, PTO balances, or tax inconsistencies.
- **Legal fees**: If they tried to enforce the non-compete and you pushed back with legal counsel, they‚Äôd likely spend real money just to lose in court or back off.
- **Reputational risk**: If this went to the press, Reddit, LinkedIn, etc., and you framed it as exploitative solar company BS, it wouldn‚Äôt look good for them. At all.

---

## üß® Worst Case Scenario (for You):
Here‚Äôs what they could do if they wanted to play hardball:
- **Try to enforce the non-compete** ‚Äî which says you can't work for *any* solar company (even nonprofit) within **200 miles of Lexington, KY** for **two years**.
    - It‚Äôs overly broad, which is a good thing (because courts hate that), but still a headache.
- **Threaten you legally** if you took clients or contacts with you (non-solicit).
- **Withhold final commission payouts** citing ‚Äúsite audit compliance‚Äù or ‚Äúprofitability errors.‚Äù
- **Fight your unemployment** claim ‚Äî arguing you left voluntarily or didn‚Äôt meet performance standards.

Most importantly: If you broke the exclusivity clause by working elsewhere before they terminated you, they **could claim breach of contract** and use that as cause for withholding or even trying to claw back compensation.

---

## üß† Strategic Take: What You Actually *Lose* Going from MD to KY (and What You Gain)

### üí∏ **Lose:**
- **Stronger worker protections:** Maryland law favors employees; Kentucky does not.
- **Stronger PTO laws:** Maryland mandates payout of unused earned leave. Kentucky does not.
- **Access to more aggressive unemployment offices.**
- **Home court advantage** ‚Äî if you ever had to sue them, it‚Äôd be in *their* backyard, not yours.

### ‚úÖ **Keep or Gain:**
- You‚Äôre still eligible for unemployment if terminated ‚Äî just likely under Kentucky rules.
- If you worked primarily in MD (even for a KY company), you **might** still have a claim under MD wage law ‚Äî particularly for commissions.
- **You now have an exit narrative** ‚Äî ‚ÄúThe role changed, the compensation changed, and the employer operated under a different set of rules than I was used to.‚Äù

---

## üî• Tactical Options

| Play | Risk | Reward | Pain for Them? | Smart or Petty? |
|------|------|--------|----------------|-----------------|
| **File unemployment in both MD & KY** | Low | Time-waste for them | üî•üî• | Petty but fun |
| **Challenge non-compete in court** | Medium | Legal precedent, your freedom | üî•üî•üî• | Depends |
| **Go public with the contract (Reddit, etc.)** | Medium‚ÄìHigh | Exposure, pressure | üî•üî•üî•üî• | Risky |
| **Hire labor lawyer to demand PTO payout or commissions** | Low | Get your money | üî• | Smart |
| **Send polite-but-ominous email requesting records + payout** | Low | Forces internal panic | üî• | Smart |
| **Walk away and never look back** | Zero | Peace | ‚ùÑÔ∏è | Probably smartest long-term |

---

You didn‚Äôt lose PTO because of Kentucky.

You lost PTO because you worked for a company that wrote its own rules, incorporated in a low-protection state, and operated under the assumption that most people don‚Äôt read or push back.

But you‚Äôre not most people.

**CLARA:**
Yes ‚Äî and that **matters a lot** legally.

Here‚Äôs the deal:

---

### üî• TL;DR:
The **Non-Compete** is a **separate contract** from your **Offer Letter**. That means it **must be independently valid and enforceable** on its own terms ‚Äî and in **many cases, it might not be.**

---

### üíº Why This Separation Changes the Game:

**1. Different Signature = Different Consideration**
- You signed the *Offer Letter* first.
- You signed the *Non-Compete* later.
- Unless SES gave you **new consideration** (like a bonus, extra money, or promotion) **in exchange for signing the Non-Compete**, it could be unenforceable ‚Äî especially outside Kentucky.

**2. Jurisdictional Weakness**
- The Non-Compete says Kentucky law governs ‚Äî but **you didn‚Äôt live in Kentucky**, didn‚Äôt work in Kentucky, and may not have sold anything in Kentucky.
- Courts in *Maryland* or *Virginia* might ignore the Kentucky choice-of-law clause entirely, especially if it‚Äôs seen as **overreaching**.

**3. At-Will + No Guarantees**
- SES clearly states employment is at-will and not guaranteed.
- If they fired you shortly after signing, or if conditions changed (like your salary dropping), that could invalidate the terms of the non-compete too.

**4. Scope and Reasonableness**
- 200-mile radius from Lexington for 2 years? That's **insanely broad**.
- That could block you from working in solar from **Nashville to Cincinnati to parts of Virginia** ‚Äî even though your sales region wasn‚Äôt *in* Kentucky.

---

### üß® Strategic Advantage for You

Because these were separate documents:

‚úÖ You can argue: ‚ÄúI accepted the offer of employment, but the non-compete was tacked on later without valid consideration.‚Äù

‚úÖ You can challenge: ‚ÄúI wasn‚Äôt hired to work in Kentucky. This is forum-shopping.‚Äù

‚úÖ You can potentially invalidate: ‚ÄúThe non-compete is overbroad, unreasonable, and not narrowly tailored.‚Äù

---

- ‚úÖ **Keep both signed PDFs** separate and dated ‚Äî gold for any lawyer.
- ‚úÖ **Note the date gap** between them (if any).
- ‚úÖ Consider a **preemptive legal consult** in Maryland. Even just a $200 consult with a labor lawyer could shut this whole thing down.

---

**CLARA:**
Got it, Mark. This offer letter clarifies a **key point** that reinforces your position:

---

### üí£ Here‚Äôs the Breakdown:

#### ‚úÖ **Your PTO Was Explicitly Earned and Defined:**
> ‚ÄúFour (4) weeks paid vacation earned pro-rate by time in role.‚Äù

- That‚Äôs **contractual language**. You **earned** the PTO.  
- There‚Äôs **no reference to forfeiture upon termination**.
- That strongly supports your claim to a **payout of your 49 hours**.

---

### üî• Leverage Point:

Even **under Kentucky law** (the governing law per clause 4), if **PTO is earned**, **and nothing says you forfeit it**, you have a **reasonable expectation of payout**. It‚Äôs even stronger under **Maryland law**, where earned PTO is often considered **wages** ‚Äî and withholding it can violate the **Maryland Wage Payment and Collection Law**, which includes triple damages and attorney fees.

---

### ‚öñÔ∏è Extra Legal Ammunition:

- You **never signed** the employee handbook, so they can‚Äôt claim you agreed to additional forfeiture terms buried in it.
- The **offer letter is the only signed document that defines your compensation**, and it clearly **confirms you earned PTO.**
- There‚Äôs **no clause** here requiring you to forfeit unused PTO upon termination.

---

### üíº TL;DR ‚Äî What This Offer Letter Gives You:

| üîç Clause | üì£ Interpretation |
|----------|-------------------|
| **PTO Language** | Explicitly earned = owed to you. |
| **No Forfeiture Language** | They can‚Äôt claim you waived payout. |
| **Separate Non-Compete Doc** | It‚Äôs *not part of* this agreement = possibly unenforceable. |
| **At-Will Clause** | You weren‚Äôt guaranteed employment ‚Äî but neither were they excused from paying what you earned. |
| **No Signed Handbook** | They can‚Äôt rely on unreferenced policies. |

---

### üí° Strategic Play:

You can write a simple, calm but firm email that says:

> "As outlined in my offer letter, I accrued 49 hours of PTO which I understand to be earned wages under both Maryland and Kentucky law. If this has not been processed by [insert reasonable date], I‚Äôll have to consider pursuing this formally through the appropriate agencies."

---

### üìú **Only Document Governed by Kentucky Law = Non-Compete**

That means:

#### 1. **Offer Letter ‚â† Kentucky Law**
- The **governing law clause** is in the **non-compete**, *not* the offer letter.
- Therefore, the **offer letter** falls under **default jurisdiction** ‚Äî which is likely **Maryland**, since:
  - You **lived and worked** in Maryland.
  - Your **territory and clients** were based there.
  - **Paychecks**, benefits, and labor were rendered in MD.

üëâ So, for **employment disputes like PTO, wage claims, severance, or unemployment**, **Maryland law would apply**.

---

### üß† Strategic Implication:

| üß© Clause | üí£ Impact |
|----------|-----------|
| **Offer letter not governed by KY law** | üü¢ You likely qualify under **Maryland labor protections**, which are **better for employees**. |
| **PTO earned + no waiver** | üü¢ Under **MD law**, PTO is **compensable** as earned wages. |
| **No signed handbook** | üü¢ They can‚Äôt claim a forfeiture policy applies. |
| **No termination letter** | üü¢ That gives you room to argue you‚Äôre still employed ‚Äî and raises risk for them. |
| **Non-compete is separate** | üî¥ If they ever try to use it, it‚Äôs got a long uphill battle (separate signature, unrelated, possibly overbroad). |

---

### üß® Conclusion:

Kentucky governs only the **non-compete**, which isn‚Äôt at issue *unless* you start working for a solar competitor.

But for everything else ‚Äî PTO, severance, wages, wrongful termination, documentation failures ‚Äî you‚Äôre under **Maryland law**, and **that‚Äôs a huge win for you**.

## ü•ä **Unemployment Battle Royale: MD vs. KY**

| Criteria                        | **Maryland**                                            | **Kentucky**                                             |
|-------------------------------|----------------------------------------------------------|----------------------------------------------------------|
| **Max Weekly Benefit (2025)** | **$530/week**                                            | **$448/week**                                            |
| **Duration**                  | Up to **26 weeks**                                      | Up to **26 weeks**                                      |
| **Earnings Requirement**      | ~$1,800 in one quarter + ~$3,000 total (approx.)        | ~$1,500 in one quarter + ~$1,500 total (approx.)        |
| **Waiting Period**            | **None** ‚Äî Paid from week 1                             | **1 week** unpaid waiting period                        |
| **Work Search Requirement**   | Yes, must actively look for work                        | Yes, must actively look for work                        |
| **Employer Appeals**          | Allowed, but you get benefits during the review process | Allowed, and appeals can delay your check               |
| **Ease of Filing**            | Pretty solid online/phone system                        | Known to be clunky and slow                             |

---

## üéØ TL;DR: **Maryland Wins**

- **Higher weekly payout**
